==PAGE:1==
Classification of SAR Images
Master’s  Thesis
Paul Jacques Connetable
June 2017
Supervisor:
Henning Skriver
==PAGE:2==
Abstract
The object of this thesis is to provide a broad study of ground cover classiﬁcation using
SAR data.
Maximum likelihood classiﬁers are good at handling mutli-temporal data
compared to other classiﬁers, due to the fact that the observations of a same pixel on the
ground are uncorrelated between two time points. Therefore, the ﬁrst goal of this work
is to assess the classiﬁcation accuracy evolution of diﬀerent crop types over time with
multi-temporal data over a growing season, with maximum likelihood classiﬁers. This
can give a chart giving the optimal number of time points needed to classify a certain
crop type with a minimum wanted accuracy. The second goal is to provide a comparison
in L-band of the classiﬁcation capability of fully polarimetric, dual polarimetric and single
polarimetric data for all polarization. This work also aims to compare the usefulness for
classiﬁcation purposes of several decomposition techniques proposed for fully polarimetric
data. Finally, this work also aims at comparing the capabilities of maximum likelihood
and random forest classiﬁers for ground cover classiﬁcation.
The results of this work
could help choosing the best polarization combination, the best decomposition of fully
polarimetric data, and the best number of time points to use for obtaining a wanted
classiﬁcation accuracy on a speciﬁc crop type. Results also tend to show that random
forest classiﬁers perform better and are easier to use than maximum likelihood classiﬁers.

Chapter 1
Introduction
In classiﬁcation problems, a classiﬁcation result is deﬁned not only by the nature of the
classiﬁer, such as maximum likelihood classiﬁers or random forest classiﬁers, but also by
the attributes chosen to represent the data to classify. The attributes are the quantities
chosen to represent the data, and on which the classiﬁcation is applied. It is theoretically
possible to choose as many attributes as wanted, but it has to be kept in mind that
the more attributes used, the higher is the dimension of the problem and thus the more
resource and time consuming it becomes.
Therefore, a compromise has to be found
between the accuracy and the time needed to perform the classiﬁcation for the choice
of the number of attributes. Thus, choosing the best attributes to represent the data is
crucial for classiﬁcation purposes.
Ground cover classiﬁcation can be done using SAR data. SAR devices send electromag-
netic waves which are scattered on the ground, and receive the back scatter. The received
signal depends on the properties of the ground and therefore contain information about
it. Some techniques to extract quantities representing the information contained in the
received signals exist and provide other attributes than the direct SAR measurements.
Moreover, target decomposition theorems exist which express the scattering mechanism as
a weighted sum of independent basic scattering mechanisms, and also provide attributes
giving a base for classiﬁcation. Applying these methods can therefore give a good basis
of attributes for classiﬁcation.
Furthermore, a previous knowledge of the capabilities of classiﬁcation of the diﬀerent po-
larization combinations, used with diﬀerent types of classiﬁers, can also highlight the most
important attributes needed to classify speciﬁc types of ground cover, such as speciﬁc crop
types. Choosing the best attributes for a speciﬁc classiﬁcation goal can lead to the selec-
tion of an optimal classiﬁer, with a high accuracy and using as few attributes as possible.
This thesis aims to investigate the usefulness of as many diﬀerent attributes as possible
for ground cover classiﬁcation; may they be obtained by polarimetric decompositions,
diﬀerent polarization combinations, or multi-temporal acquisitions.
Chapter two presents diﬀerent of the most popular decompositions of SAR data. Chapter
three explains the theory about maximum likelihood classiﬁers and more speciﬁcally, the
maximum likelihood classiﬁers applied to the elements of the covariance matrix, along with
random forest classiﬁers. Chapter four is the study of a dual polarimetric multi-temporal
data set, and consists in the study of the evolution of the accuracy of maximum likelihood
11
==PAGE:14==
classiﬁers on several speciﬁc crop types with the number of time points. Finally, chapter
ﬁve is the study of another data set, which is fully polarimetric. This data set is used
to compare the usefulness of the diﬀerent decompositions for classiﬁcation, to compare
the classiﬁcations obtained on the diﬀerent subsets of the covariance matrix which could
be measured by a dual polarimetric or a single polarimetric SAR, and to compare the
capabilities of maximum likelihood classiﬁers and random forest classiﬁers.
12
==PAGE:15==
Chapter 2
Attribute selection
2.1
Decomposition of the polarimetric data
Two diﬀerent kinds of decomposition theorems exist: the coherent decomposition theo-
rems and the incoherent ones.
Coherent decomposition theorems exploit the information contained on the scattering
matrix [S], deﬁned in equation (2.1). They are used to study coherent targets, also called
pure or point targets, which means that the measured signal at the reception is the result
of a single type of scattering on the ground. Man-made structures tend to show this kind
of behaviour.
Incoherent decomposition methods are based on second order matrices such as the covari-
ance or the coherency matrix, respectively deﬁned in equations (2.6) and (2.7). They are
suited to study distributed scatterers, which cannot be studied only with the scattering
matrix. Natural scenes such as crop ﬁelds are usually composed of distributed scatter-
ers, which makes the use of incoherent target decompositions more suited for their study.
As the purpose of this work is mainly to study classiﬁcation of natural scenes and crop
ﬁelds, the emphasis has been put on incoherent decomposition theorems. Therefore, it is
possible to go from a coherent formulation of the data (the [S] matrix) to a incoherent
formulation, but it is impossible to do the contrary, since the incoherent formulation also
implies a loss of information compared to the coherent one. It is indeed impossible to
retrieve exactly the elements of the scattering matrix from the coherency or the covari-
ance matrices, as it can be seen from their deﬁnitions. As most data are obtained in the
incoherent formulation, coherent decompositions are not treated here.
Furthermore, some noise is present in the data. The most important part of that noise is
speckle noise. It is a granular, random noise, which cannot be corrected for at the mea-
surement time by the radar, but needs to be corrected for with ﬁltering and a statistical
approach of the data after its acquisition. The origin of this noise is due to the fact that
on the ground, the physical scatterers are very close to each other, and the back scatter
waves they return can interfere with each other. The signals returned add coherently,
which means that the total signals intensity depends on the relative phase of the diﬀerent
back scatters (the interference can be constructive or destructive). The reduction of this
noise is an important step before trying to classify the ground cover. Multi-looking is
13
==PAGE:16==
the ﬁrst and important step to reduce this noise. It consists in averaging neighbouring
pixels, and thus reduces the ground resolution. The multi-looked data can therefore be
expressed as an average over several pixels, and written with ⟨⟩.
SAR systems receive coherent radar signals, which are usually represented by the 2 by 2
Sinclair matrix [S]. The elements of the matrix express the change induced by the scatter-
ing mechanism on the ground, both in amplitude and phase. In this matrix, h describes
the horizontal polarization of the electromagnetic wave, and v the vertical polarization.
In the fully polarimetric case (transmitting and receiving both).

2.2
Publications comparing decomposition methods
An important number of publications have studied and compared several decomposition
theorems and the accuracy of the classiﬁcation obtained based on the said decomposition,
and obtained very interesting results.
Firstly, in [6], Cloude and Pottier describe very thoroughly the creation of the covariance,
the coherency and the Mueller matrices (another matrix representation of the representa-
tion) of a pixel. Since the Mueller matrix is not used in this thesis, it is not explained in
any more detail here, but some more information can be found in [12]. They then explain
(but do not implement or present results) a great number of decomposition theorems,
that they divide in three diﬀerent classes. The ﬁrst one is composed of decompositions
based on the Huynen decomposition, described in depth in [12]. This method is one of
the ﬁrst target decompositions proposed back in 1970 by Huynen in his PhD thesis. It
is based on the decomposition of the Mueller matrix. However, as being the precursor
of target decompositions, this decomposition presents some ﬂaws pointed out by several
articles. In particular, one has to note that this decomposition is not unique (as stated in
[6]). Even if this method has been reviewed recently, in particular in [20], it still presents
some ﬂaws, which is why it hasn’t been implemented here. The second class of target
decomposition theorems presented are the ones based on the eigenvalues and eigenvectors
of the coherency matrix. These target decomposition theorems all follow the entropy-α (
written H-α) decomposition presented by Cloude and Pottier in [7]. The most advanced
method has been implemented and is further presented in section 2.3. The paper [6], also
presents the Freeman and Durden decomposition, and its improvement done by Van Zyl
in [28]. These methods are further presented in section 2.4. Finally, [6] presents a few
15
==PAGE:18==
examples of coherent target decomposition, which are directly based on the scattering
matrix.
Another interesting paper on this matter is an ESA manual [8] presenting a few polari-
metric decompositions. This paper explains brieﬂy the diﬀerent ideas behind coherent
and incoherent decompositions. It presents coherent decompositions, which are not ( the
Pauli, the Krogager, the Cameron decompositions, along with the Touzi criterion, which
estimates if the scatterer is coherent or incoherent). It then presents the Freeman and
Durden method, the Huynen decomposition and the H-α decomposition for the incoherent
ones.
Two papers compared several classiﬁcation method based on diﬀerent decomposition
methods. In [1], Alberga compares classiﬁcation accuracies obtained with diﬀerent co-
herent decomposition methods, the direct use of the elements of the scattering matrix,
and the H-α anisotropy decomposition proposed by Cloude and Pottier in [7]. He con-
cludes that the most accurate decomposition amongst the ones tried on the test area
studied in the paper is the Krogager decomposition (the sphere-diplane-helix decomposi-
tion), for all the classiﬁcation methods tried. The same kind of work has been done in [26],
where Turkar compared the accuracies of classiﬁcation of the same classiﬁcation methods
(on another test area) based on the Krogager decomposition, and several decompositions
based on the Freeman and Durden decomposition, explained in [9]. These decompositions
include the Freeman and Durden one, the Van Zyl decomposition proposed in [28], and
the four element decomposition proposed by Yamaguchi in [31]. All these decompositions
are explained further in sections 2.4, 2.5 and 2.6 respectively. For this second test area,
the Krogager decomposition has a performance close to the one found by Alberga in [1].
It appears that the decomposition giving the best classiﬁcation accuracy is the Van Zyl
decomposition, followed closely by the Freeman and Durden method. The Yamaguchi
decomposition performs worse than these two, but still slightly better than the Krogager
decomposition, if only the ﬁrst three components are kept (the same as in the Freeman and
Durden decomposition). Finally, the four components Yamaguchi decomposition gives the
worst classiﬁcation accuracy in this paper. The authors explain this bad performance by
noting that the Yamaguchi four elements decomposition is aimed towards improving the
classiﬁcation of more urban areas, while the test area comports more natural targets and
classes.
A similar study has been carried out in [32].
In this paper, Zhang implements and
compares the strengths and weaknesses of six decompositions. Three incoherent decom-
positions are treated there. The ﬁrst one is the Freeman and Durden decomposition. It
is indicated as performing extremely well for distributed scatterers, and giving a good
analysis of the scattering mechanisms on natural targets, but not being able to distin-
guish between forest and man-made buildings. The second method is the one proposed
by Moriyama in [22]. This method performs well for urban areas, and poorly on natural
areas. Finally, the last method investigated here is the four-element decomposition pro-
posed by Yamaguchi in [31]. It is in between the Freeman and Durden and the Moriyama
decompositions, giving a good average performance on natural and urban areas. Since it
appears here that Moriyama’s decomposition is not well suited for natural targets classi-
ﬁcation, this decomposition has not been implemented in this work.
Finally, a great number of decomposition techniques are explained and implemented in
[2] and [14]. In both these papers, the authors compare the accuracies of all the classiﬁers
16
==PAGE:19==
based on the attributes obtained with the decompositions presented, with a speciﬁc clas-
siﬁcation method for each (a neural network classiﬁer, the Multi Layer Perceptron, in [2]
and both a minimum distance and a maximum likelihood classiﬁer in [14]). They both
provide a summary of the results they obtained as a graph presenting the overall accuracy
of all the methods tried. These graphs are presented here ﬁgure 2.1. In this ﬁgure the
accuracies of maximum likelihood classiﬁcations based on the attributes obtained either
with diﬀerent decomposition methods, or with diﬀerent ways of transforming the imme-
diate data obtained are compared. Some decompositions or techniques presented here are
not relevant in the scope of this thesis, since they are not implemented, and have not been
presented here. Articles giving further explanations about these techniques are indicated
in table 2.1, which summarizes the main decomposition techniques existing today, in a
non exhaustive way. One can see in this ﬁgure that it appears that generally speaking,
incoherent methods seem to perform slightly better than coherent methods in terms of
overall accuracy, except the H-α decomposition. Furthermore, in [2], Alberga presents
the user and producer accuracies for each class and each decomposition. It appears that
unlike what one could expect and the results obtained by Zhang in [32], the Moriyama
decomposition performs slightly better than the Freeman and Durden decomposition on
natural targets. A very important point which can be seen on these arrays of accuracies is
that even if some decompositions appear to give better overall accuracies and may seem
just superior to others, they all give diﬀerent accuracies on speciﬁc classes. This means
that a decomposition, even having a smaller overall accuracy than another, can perform
better on speciﬁc classes. Thus, it shows the importance of trying a great number of
diﬀerent decompositions, in order to have a robust basis of attributes able to diﬀerentiate
as well as possible all classes.

In all the following sections, where some decomposition methods are explained in more
depth, the notation of the explanation is always based on the paper cited, to keep the
subject directly understandable if the reader wants to read the paper. It means that
between two diﬀerent decomposition, the variables not introduced beforehand (as the
scattering matrix or the covariance matrix for example) change.
2.3
Entropy-α-anisotropy decomposition
This ﬁrst decomposition has been proposed by Cloude and Pottier in [7], and is also
explained in [15].
It has been designed both as a decomposition and a classiﬁcation
technique, but only the decomposition part will be treated here, for a reason explained
later on. The results obtained when applying this decomposition to a fully polarimetric
data set are presented section 5.3.1.
2.3.1
Fully polarimetric
The technique has primarly been designed for fully polarimetric data. It is based on the
eigenvector and eigenvalue decomposition of the coherency matrix T of a pixel. If we
note the three eigenvalues λi and the corresponding eigenvectors ei, we can rewrite the
coherency matrix, as it is Hermitian, as
19
==PAGE:22==
T = λ1e1e∗T
1 + λ2e2e∗T
2 + λ3e3e∗T
3
(2.10)
with
ei = eiφi[cos(αi) sin(αi)cos(βi)eiδi sin(αi)sin(βi)eiγi]T.
(2.11)
For a speciﬁc eigenvector, αi represents the type of scattering: from 0◦representing a
surface scattering, 45◦representing a canopy scattering (randomly oriented scattering), to
90◦representing a double-bounce scattering. It is therefore a very important variable for
scattering classiﬁcation. The other angle components are usually not used in classiﬁcation:
while βi can be used in some methods to analyze ground slopes, the physical meaning of
the other angles is not well understood [24]. The entropy of the scattering is deﬁned by:
H =
3
X
i=1
−Pi log3(Pi) with Pi =
λi
P
j λj
(2.12)
H deﬁnes the randomness of the scattering on the ground, and is also very good at
diﬀerentiating the diﬀerent types of scattering.
A mean α angle for the scattering is
created, and corresponds to the weighted sum of the α angles with the importance of
each eigenvector, and deﬁned as
ˆα =
3
X
i=1
Piαi.
(2.13)
This mean angle represents the mean mechanism of the ground scattering. The mean
alpha angle and the entropy were the only two elements introduced in this decomposition
when it was ﬁrst proposed in [7]. With only these two elements, Cloude and Pottier
proposed an unsupervised classiﬁcation based on the decomposition of the H-alpha plane.
This plane representation and its decomposition into nine zones as it is presented in
[7] is presented ﬁgure 2.2. This shows that this decomposition gives results which are
very well understood and linked directly to physical properties of the scattering. The
unsupervised classiﬁcation proposed by Cloude and Pottier is directly obtained through
the decomposition of the plane shown in the ﬁgure.
The classiﬁcation scheme is not
investigated here for two reasons. This classiﬁcation only takes into account these two
parameters, and cannot be used more generally with other attributes. Moreover, such
a decomposition can be obtained in an optimized way for supervised classiﬁcation with
random forest classiﬁers, giving better results since it is more data driven. The random
forest classiﬁer can also be used with as many attributes as wanted and still be trained. It
therefore gives more data driven results, and can be used in a more general way. This kind
of classiﬁer is further explained in chapter 3. Since random forest classiﬁers are studied
in this thesis, there is no need to investigate the classiﬁcation scheme proposed by Cloude
and Pottier, which is in fact equivalent to an already trained random forest classiﬁer only
on these two attributes.
20
==PAGE:23==
Figure 2.2: The H-α plane and its decomposition for unsupervised classiﬁcation as pro-
posed by Cloude and Pottier. This image is extracted from [7]. The meaning of the zones
in this classiﬁcation is not explained in more depth here, as it is not studied in this thesis.
More explanations about this classiﬁcation are provided in [7].
Finally, a third component has been introduced to improve this classiﬁcation, which is
the anisotropy, noted A. This new component estimates the relative importance of the
two last eigenvalues, and therefore, of the two last scattering mechanisms.
This new
parameter gives further information complementary to the entropy. When the entropy is
rather high, it is unclear whether the total scattering involves only two or all of the three
basic mechanisms represented by this decomposition. The anisotropy can be obtained
with
A = λ2 −λ3
λ2 + λ3
.
(2.14)
This decomposition is well known and one of the most important and largely used ones.
However, it provides attributes which are less suited to classiﬁcation than other decom-
positions presented later. A further improvement is proposed in [2] of this decomposition:
the addition of the total power would greatly help the classiﬁcation scheme according to
the authors.
2.3.2
Dual-polarization

2.4
Freeman and Durden method
This decomposition method is explained in [9], and is cited in almost every publica-
tion cited above comparing decompositions. This method was created to give a directly
intelligible decomposition of the signal with a physical meaning, while most other de-
composition theorems give mathematically extracted quantities, which are more diﬃcult
to relate to the physical scattering happening on the ground. As pointed out in section
2.2, this decomposition method gives attributes allowing an understandable and reliable
classiﬁcation of natural targets, but it is less suited for urban areas. It is based on the
decomposition of the covariance matrix in three matrices representing the three basic
scattering mechanisms: volume, surface and double-bounce scattering. Figure 2.3 shows
the physical representation of the three basic scattering mechanisms. Each one of them
is associated with a covariance matrix corresponding to the theoretical pure scattering
mechanism in question.

is represented the volume scattering, on the middle one the double-bounce scattering and
surface scattering is on the bottom image. This image comes from [9].

In order to keep this decomposition unscaled to the total power, and be able to train a
classiﬁer on these three elements which could be used more generally on other images, one
can express the three powers in percentage of the total power. The Freeman and Durden
method then provides the fraction of each scattering mechanism involved on the ground
scattering.
2.5
Van Zyl decomposition
Though giving a very similar kind of decomposition as the Freeman and Durden method
presented immediately above, the method presented by Van Zyl in [27] and later in [28]
is based on a diﬀerent approach of the problem. In this decomposition, the focus was
put rather on the number of reﬂections of the signal on the ground (if it is even, odd or
comes from randomly oriented small cylinders). This slightly diﬀerent approach is based
on the extraction of information from the covariance matrix C in a very similar way as
information is extracted from the coherency matrix T in the H-α-A decomposition ex-
plained in section 2.3. Just as Freeman and Durden, and as shown in [23], the assumption
of azimuthal symmetry is made, meaning that ⟨S∗
hvShh⟩and ⟨ShvS∗
vv⟩are 0. Thus, the

the model matrix can be chosen by looking at the sign of the imaginary part of this mean.

then fc and fv are set to 0, along with the powers of these mechanisms, and the double

This decomposition works better than the ones presented before for urban areas, but as
shown in [32], it performs worse (in overall accuracy) over natural targets.
2.7
Van Zyl Non Negative Eigenvalues Decomposi-
tion (NNED)
In 2011, Van Zyl in [29] revisited the decompositions proposed by Durden and Freeman
and Yamaguchi, and proposed a further improvement.
He indeed points out that in
both these decompositions, because there is one more unknown than equations, a value is
arbitrarily chosen for either α or β depending on the most important mechanism between
surface and double bounce scattering. Furthermore, several diﬀerent models can be chosen
to describe the canopy scattering, as it cannot always be modelled by a uniform canopy of
thin cylinders. This results in an overestimation of the volume scattering in some cases.
Van Zyl proposes here a new way to decompose the data solving these two ﬂaws. It is
based on the important similarity of the obtained decompositions between the method
proposed by Freeman and Durden explained in 2.4 and his own explained in section 2.5.
The ﬁrst modiﬁcation brought here is the way to ﬁnd the volume scattering power. It is
the ﬁrst power estimated in this decomposition. This new way of determining the volume
scattering power is also based on a diﬀerent approach than Yamaguchi’s one to select a
canopy model. For a given canopy model Ccanopy, a coeﬃcient called a here is applied to
this model. The ﬁrst stage of the decomposition can be written

The goal here is to ﬁnd, for a speciﬁc model, the a coeﬃcient which both minimizes the

This ﬁrst step ensures that all powers in the decompositions will be positive. The second

explained above in 2.5 can be used to estimate the importance of the other scattering
29
==PAGE:32==
mechanisms.
It has indeed be seen that this decomposition gives a decomposition of
the scattering mechanism in terms of odd and even number of reﬂections, and a third
component which is closer to a volume scattering (2.25). The odd number of reﬂections
is very close to the surface scattering mechanism, and the even number of reﬂections is
very close to the double bounce scattering. If we apply this decomposition to the matrix
Cremain obtained in (2.33) after subtracting the canopy part, this decomposition provides
accurate estimations for surface and double bounce scattering, since the third term of
the decomposition is going to be very small compared to them. Therefore, the Van Zyl
decomposition is then applied to Cremain. Finally, we can write the decomposition as
C = aCcanopy + λoddCodd + λevenCeven + λdiffCdiff.
(2.36)
Here, the coeﬃcients λodd and λeven and their corresponding model matrices are obtained
directly from the eigenvector decomposition proposed by Van Zyl in [28] in 1992. The term
λdiffCdiff represents the leftover power, which is not explained by the decomposition. It
should be as small as possible. This is this parameter which leads the selection of the best
canopy model. The best canopy model is the one which minimizes this leftover power.
The powers as usual are derived by multiplying the coeﬃcient of a scattering mechanism
with the trace of the model matrix.

Chapter 3
Classiﬁcation methods
The purpose of this chapter is to present the diﬀerent classiﬁers implemented and which
results are compared in this work. In this project, only supervised classiﬁers have been
implemented and compared. Supervised classiﬁers are very widely used for all kind of
classiﬁcation, and are very well performing.
Supervised classiﬁcation requires the knowledge of the diﬀerent classes by the user. In
this case, the user determines which are the diﬀerent classes in the data set, and provides
for each class a subset of the observations to the classiﬁer to determine the diﬀerent
parameters needed for the classiﬁer to work (for example the mean or the variance of the
observations). This subset is called a training set. Usually, part of the data known to
belong to the class is kept away from the training set, and is used later on. This second
set is called the test set. Once the classiﬁer is trained on all classes, it is tried on all the
data, and its accuracy can be assessed with the test sets. It is indeed possible to study
the classiﬁcation of the data made by the classiﬁer on the test sets and compare it with
the reality. This study is usually presented as the confusion matrix for SAR data. If
the classiﬁcation is not judged satisfactory, the user can make new iterations with some
adjustments such as merging some classes together.
Unsupervised classiﬁcation works a bit diﬀerently.
It is completely automatized, and
usually only needs as an input the number of diﬀerent classes wanted. A large number
of diﬀerent algorithms exist for unsupervised classiﬁcation, but they usually work as an
iterative procedure. For example, a very used unsupervised classiﬁer for SAR data is
presented in [15]. The diﬀerent classes are ﬁrst deﬁned based on the diﬀerent regions
developed by Cloude and Pottier in their decomposition explained section 2.3 and shown
ﬁgure 2.2. Then, the data is classed according to the classiﬁer, and the class centers
moved to the mean of the observations in the class. The iteration stops when the class
centers are moving below a threshold deﬁned by the user or if the number of iterations
becomes bigger than an arbitrary value deﬁned beforehand.
A great diﬀerence between these two kinds of classiﬁers is that supervied classiﬁers give
a classiﬁcation based on classes which make sense to the user, while unsupervised clas-
siﬁcations can give classes very diﬃcult to interpret for a human, since they are based
purely on the statistical proximity between observations. For ground cover classiﬁcation,
supervised methods are generally more used for this reason, since the diﬀerent types of
ground cover that one wishes to class are already known beforehand.
31
==PAGE:34==
3.1
Maximum likelihood classiﬁcation
3.1.1
Theory
Maximum likelihood classiﬁers are very well known and used in machine learning in
general, and for ground cover classiﬁcation with SAR data more speciﬁcally. It requires the
previous knowledge of the statistical distribution of the data to class, and the extraction
for each diﬀerent class of the parameters describing the class distribution with the training
sets. With a maximum likelihood classiﬁer, an observation u is classed in class ωm if
p(ωm|u) ≥p(ωj|u) for all j ̸= m.
(3.1)
If we use Bayes’ rule, we can rewrite this probability as
p(ωm|u) = p(u|ωm)p(ωm)
p(u)
.
(3.2)
The probability p(ωm) represents the a-priori probability of class m. For a given obser-
vation u, the probability p(u) is the same for all classes, and we can therefore rewrite
equation (3.1) as
u ∈ωm if p(u|ωm)p(ωm) > p(u|ωj)p(ωj),
for all j ̸= m.
(3.3)
For using this classiﬁcation method on SAR data, all we need now is an expression of the
probability density functions of the distributions of the diﬀerent variables obtained with
SAR.
3.1.2
Full covariance matrix
The full covariance matrix follows a Wishart distribution, as it is shown in [19] and in
[18]. For a Wishart distribution, deﬁned by a three by three covariance matrix C, the
probability of a three by three matrix A is
p(A) = |A|n−q exp[−Tr(C−1A)]
K(n, q)|C|n
(3.4)
where
K(n, q) = π
1
2 q(q−1)Γ(n) . . . Γ(n −q + 1);
(3.5)
32
==PAGE:35==
and
– n is the ENL;
– q is the dimension of the matrix. For the full covariance matrix, q = 3;
– Tr is the trace of the matrix;
– Γ is the gamma function.
In order to bring simpliﬁcation to the computation, a distance measure between a matrix
and a Wishart distribution has been developed, based on this distribution. This distance
measure is vastly used in the literature, as it keeps almost all of the information contained
in the probability density function (PDF), and thus gives results almost as good as when
using the whole PDF, while being quicker to compute. This distance is actually the one
used for all classiﬁcations based on the Wishart distribution, as in [17] or [18].
This
distance, between a covariance matrix Z and a class ωm which class center is Cm is
d(Z, ωm) = log(|Cm|) + Tr(C−1
m Z).
(3.6)
In this situation, the parameter estimated in the training set is simply the average covari-
ance matrix of the observations. In this case, when using the distance from an observation
to a class, the classiﬁer is no longer exactly a maximum likelihood classiﬁer, but a min-
imum distance classiﬁer. The diﬀerence in classiﬁcation between a maximum likelihood
classiﬁer and a minimum distance one is fairly small, and both are very close to each other.
In the rest of the report, the Wishart classiﬁcation is referred as a maximum likelihood
classiﬁer, though it is in reality a minimum distance one, since the two are very close.
3.1.3
Two by two matrix subset of the covariance matrix
It is not unusual that the data is obtained with a dual polarization SAR instrument,
and not a fully polarimetric one. In this case, only a subset of the covariance matrix is
available. In this subsection, we study the conﬁguration where the two intensities and the
two diagonal elements are known (therefore giving a two by two matrix). This case is very
straightforward, since the same PDF can be used as for the full covariance matrix case
(given equation (3.4)), with using q = 2. Therefore, the same distance measure as before
can be used, using two by two matrices. The classiﬁer based on this subset is refered as
reduced Wishart classiﬁer in the rest of the thesis.
3.1.4
Dual intensity
The data obtained by a dual polarization SAR can also give only two intensities, without
the diagonal elements. In this case, the distribution followed by two intensities R1 and
R2, as given in [19] and [18], is a dual chi square distribution

Single intensity

3.1.6
Multi-temporal data
Finally, when some data is acquired on the same area at diﬀerent dates, the assumption
has been made that there is no correlation between observations for diﬀerent time points.
This assumption is very used for SAR data and its classiﬁcation, and the most known
and best performing maximum likelihood classiﬁers are also using this assumption. It is
34
==PAGE:37==
for example made in [17]. This can be summarized with two uncorrelated variables x1
and x2 by
P(x1, x2) = P(x1|x2)P(x2) = P(x1)P(x2).
(3.10)
3.2
Random forest classiﬁer
Random forest classiﬁers are very powerful classiﬁers, widely used in machine learning.
They are also used for SAR ground cover classiﬁcation, as in [21].
In this paper, a
random forest classiﬁer is used for classiﬁcation of ground cover based on a multidate
data set. The classiﬁer is there used to study the relative importance of the diﬀerent
attributes of the problem for classiﬁcation, aiming to reduce the number of feature for the
classiﬁcation while keeping the best possible classiﬁcation accuracy. This theory section
has been written based on the explanations written in [10] and the book of the course
Introduction to machine learning and data mining in DTU [25].
3.2.1
Classiﬁcation trees
Classiﬁcation trees are a type of classiﬁer, which are conceptually simple but a very
powerful supervised classiﬁer. They were ﬁrst used by Hunt in [11] in 1966. The algorithm
they use is called Hunt’s algorithm, and is fairly simple. Let’s call attributes the diﬀerent
variables representing an observation to class, and assume that a training set has been
given to the tree classiﬁer. At each node of the tree, we subdivide the set in two subsets.
This means that the classiﬁcation tree ﬁrst splits the training set in two subsets, and then
recursively splits each subset obtained in two other subsets. It continues splitting in two
the subsets until it reaches a stopping criterion. At each step, the best possible split is
determined among a great number of possible splits by choosing the best attribute and
threshold to separate the set in two. The choice is made between all the possible splits
by choosing the one which maximizes the purity gain ∆
∆= I(r) −
2
X
k=1
N(vk)
N(r) I(vk)
(3.11)
if we write I(r) the impurity of the root, I(vk) the impurity of the kth branch, N(r) the
number of elements in the root and N(vk) the number of elements in the kth branch.
There are three main impurity measures used for classiﬁcation trees. In this work, the
impurity measure used is the Gini index. It is the most used of the three diﬀerent impurity
measures, and is a good approximation of the entropy, while still taking less computational
time than using the real entropy measure, thus leading to a lower computational time
required to train a classiﬁer on a data set. The Gini impurity of a set is given by
I(v) = 1 −
C
X
c=1
p(c|v)2
(3.12)
35
==PAGE:38==
where C is the number of diﬀerent classes, and p(c|v) is the number of elements belonging
to the class c in the set. Usually, the stopping criteria in a classiﬁcation tree is its depth,
a minimum set size and a minimum impurity in a set.
3.2.2
Ensemble methods
It is known that decision tree tend to over-ﬁt the data. A ﬁrst idea has been to apply
bagging to classiﬁcation trees.
Bagging consists in splitting the original training set
in several smaller sets, and train a diﬀerent classiﬁer on each of these sets. For data
classiﬁcation, a classiﬁcation is done by each of these classiﬁers and the ﬁnal classiﬁcation
result is obtained by majority voting. Though this technique works very well while using
diﬀerent classiﬁers, it does not really improve the classiﬁcation if only classiﬁcation are
trained. The diﬀerent trees will indeed be extremely correlated. Breiman proposed in
20001 in [4] a technique to overcome this problem, which forms the basis of the random
tree classiﬁer. At each node, instead of applying Hunt’s algorithm on all the attributes,
the tree should only consider a subset of the m < M of the attributes. This creates less
correlated trees, and allows the increase in the number of trees to signiﬁcantly improve
classiﬁcation.
It is important to note that when cross-validation is performed on the training of a
random forest classiﬁer, the out of bag error is directly based on the fraction of miss-
classiﬁed observations. It means that small classes have less weight than big classes in
the generalization error, and that the classiﬁer will tend to ﬁt better the bigger classes.
Therefore, when giving the classiﬁer training data, one has to ensure that all the classes
are of roughly the same size in the training set. In order to do so, two methods can be
used. Oversampling can be for smaller classes, that is replicating some of the data until
a satisfactory size is obtained. One must be careful not to over-sample a class too much,
for there is a risk of over-ﬁtting the training data on this class. The other way around,
where only a random subset of the observations is selected, is called undersampling, and
is used for bigger classes. In the same way, undersampling too much can lead to a loss of
information. A compromise must therefore be found for ﬁnding the adequate number of
observations each class should have in the training set.
36
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 4
Classiﬁcation on Sentinel-1 data set
4.1
The data set
Sentinel 1 is an ESA satellite, which provides SAR measurements in C-band (5.405 GHz).
This wavelength is ideal for sea ice surveillance, but only penetrates the top layers of vege-
tation, which does not make it the best tool for vegetation covers classiﬁcation. Moreover,
the data acquired on land is not fully polarimetric, but has a dual polarization. The signal
is transmitted in a single polarization, here vertical (V), and received both in vertical and
horizontal (H) polarizations. In this project, the goal is to study the classiﬁcation over
an area of Denmark. More speciﬁcally, the focus is of the project is to compare diﬀerent
classiﬁers and their accuracies on crop ﬁelds, and at the same time to assess the accuracy
improvement with the number of images. Finally, the area studied should be in the island
of Zealand, in Denmark, as it is the zone covered by the radar data used for this project.
The ﬁrst action was to choose an area of interest where the classiﬁcation could be done.
This area should contain as much variety as possible in crop ﬁelds, and be in the island of
Zealand. The Danish Agriculture and Fishing agency provides online shape ﬁles over the
whole country associated with the kind of crops growing in each ﬁeld. These data can be
found on their website at the address http://lfst.dk/landbrug/kort-og-markblokke/
markkort-og-markblokke/. The work presented here is based on the data provided for
2016. The shape ﬁle can be opened in QGIS, and the ﬁelds can be classed in function of
the crop type. An overview of the whole Denmark with such a colour classiﬁcation of the
ﬁelds based on the crop type is presented ﬁgure 4.1. After investigation, the interest area
chosen is an area around Stenløse, in the island of Zealand. It has been selected since it is
the area on the island presenting the highest crop diversity, which is an important feature
for this classiﬁcation work. The area is around 22 km by 22 km, and presents a large
variety of crop ﬁelds. The colour classiﬁcation of the crop ﬁelds on this area, obtained
from Qgis, is presented ﬁgure 4.2. An optical image of this exact same area is presented
ﬁgure 4.3.
The data studied here are composed of twelve diﬀerent images for twelve time points,
covering the growing season between the seventh of March to the 29th of July. It has been
preprocessed by DHI Gras, and for each image a pixel is deﬁned with two intensities in
dB, S2
vv and S2
vh. In the preprocessing stage, the data has also been speckle ﬁltered.
37
==PAGE:40==
Figure 4.1: Crop ﬁelds over whole Denmark, obtained with the shape ﬁle on Qgis.
Figure 4.2: Crop ﬁelds over the study area, with a colour classiﬁcation for the 142 diﬀerent
crop types registered in the shape ﬁle.
38
==PAGE:41==
Figure 4.3: Optical image of the study zone, including names of major places.
4.2
Equivalent number of looks (ENL)
The matlab code used to produce the results shown in this section is presented appendix
A.3.1.
One of the ﬁrst elements needed when analyzing SAR data is the ENL. The ENL is a
measure of averaging between neighbouring pixels and hence of noise removal in SAR
data. As speckle ﬁltering decreases the noise and therefore increase the ENL, the ENL of
the data is no longer the one documented for the instrument by ESA. An estimation of
the ENL needs to be done on the data set before any further study, as a great number of
applications require its knowledge. On this data set, we have access to the ground truth,
and therefore know the diﬀerent ﬁelds on the area. All these ﬁelds are homogeneous areas,
on which an estimate of the ENL can be computed. Several methods exist to evaluate
an image’s ENL. The most reﬁned ones, as described in [3] make the use of a moving
window over the whole image, pixel by pixel, and use the distributions obtained to obtain
an accurate estimation of the ENL. The drawback of this kind of technique is that it
requires a lot of computations and takes a lot of time. Since we have here the knowledge
of a great number of homogeneous areas, the simple ENL estimation based on these areas
would also give a very good estimation of the ENL, with way less operations. The ENL
estimate over an homogeneous area is obtained with
ˆLe = E[I]2
V [I]
(4.1)
39
==PAGE:42==
where I is the intensity of a polarimetric channel. At ﬁrst, a study of the size of the
ﬁelds in pixels in the area has been done. The histogram of the size of ﬁelds in pixels
is presented ﬁgure 4.4. It can be seen from this histogram that the size of ﬁelds almost
follows an exponential distribution, with an important number of ﬁelds containing a small
amount of pixels. Fields containing a too small amount of pixels are likely to give a biased
estimation of the ENL, since a too small subset is not enough to obtain a robust variance
of the intensity over all the pixels. Thus, a selection must be made on the minimum
size of the ﬁelds to select for estimating an ENL, which both leaves only homogeneous
regions big enough to have an accurate estimation and enough ﬁelds to have access to a
good statistical representation of the ENL on the image. The threshold of 500 pixels has
been arbitrarily selected for this purpose. This threshold appears to be satisfactory, as
it selects 1403 ﬁelds out of the 6477 ﬁelds in the data set, and as 500 pixels is a large
enough subset to give a good estimation of the ENL on a speciﬁc ﬁeld.
0
1000
2000
3000
4000
5000
6000
Number of pixels
0

Number of fields
Figure 4.4: Histogram the number of pixels in all ﬁelds over the studied area.
Using this subset of ﬁelds, an ENL estimate has been evaluated for each polarization and
each time point, using the mean of the ENL estimations on the ﬁelds for this date and
polarization. In addition, the histograms of the ENL estimates on each ﬁeld for each
polarization and time point have been created. Figure 4.5 shows these histograms for the
ﬁrst and last time point, for both polarizations. The histograms on this ﬁgure show that
the ﬁelds’ ENLs are close to following a Gaussian distribution. The mean and the median
of a Gaussian distribution are the same, so taking the mean of all the ENL estimates
on all the ﬁelds gives a good approximation of the real ENL. All the ENL estimates are
presented in table 4.1. The ENL estimates obtained with the mean of the ﬁelds’ ENLs
vary between 32 and 47, and have a mean of 39.4.

4.3
Selection of classes
4.3.1
Reduction of the number of classes
The ﬁrst challenge with this data set was to reduce the number of classes to a reasonable
amount. The data provided in the shape ﬁle indeed contained an extremely high amount
of diﬀerent classes, with sometimes very little diﬀerence between them. In the smaller
area studied were registered 142 diﬀerent classes. A histogram of the total area covered
per class is presented ﬁgure 4.6. It appears that a great number of classes actually cover a
very small area, and hence are almost insigniﬁcant. In order to select the most important
classes, only the classes covering an area over a threshold ﬁxed at 350 arbitrary units have
been kept. This threshold has been selected manually after studying the histogram, as it
makes a good distinction between the most important classes and the dozen of smaller
classes which area stands around 300 AU and appear less important for the classiﬁcation
purposes. This leaves a total of 12 classes, shown in table 4.2. All the other classes are
merged together in a super class called ”Other ﬁelds”. This image is obtained with the
code presented appendix A.3.1.

The classes presenting bi-modal distributions must therefore be subdivided.
The two
modes appear very clearly on all histograms, excepted for the Spring oats class, which
histogram is presented ﬁgure 4.7a. The ideal conﬁguration would be that for all classes,
the two modes represent completely diﬀerent ﬁelds, which would have been mislabelled
in the shape ﬁle, and not a within-class variation. The way chosen to extract points from
the minor distributions has been to set a threshold on the value of the pixel in dB in the
right polarization and date, corresponding to the histograms ﬁgure 4.7. These threshold
values have been selected manually after study of the aforementioned histograms. Figure
4.8 shows these classes presenting a bi-modal distribution for at least one polarization
and time point. For each class, are represented in dark blue all the points which are not
44
==PAGE:47==
in the class; in yellow, the points belonging to the main mode, relatively to the threshold
used; and in red, all the points of the class belonging to the minor mode, according to
the same threshold. For all classes, except the Spring oats one, there was a very clear
diﬀerence between the two modes in the distributions, and it can seen in ﬁgure 4.8 that
the pixels belonging to the minor mode are actually always gathered into speciﬁc ﬁelds,
making homogeneous ﬁelds both for the pixels belonging to the minor mode and the main
mode of the distribution. This is a good result, as explained immediately above, since
it means that this is most probably a miss-labelling of the ﬁeld. For the Spring oats
class, the two modes were not well separated, thus the distinction is less clear, since the
threshold will mix pixels from both modes. It is then normal that the ﬁelds belonging to
the minor distribution appear less clearly homogeneous as they do for the other classes.
From the histogram ﬁgure 4.7a, it is possible to evaluate that the minor mode has a mean
value around -20 dB, and that it should contain a rather small amount of pixels. Three
ﬁelds particularly stand out by presenting more than half of the pixels belonging to the
minor mode, as it can be seen ﬁgure 4.8a. With the results obtained for the other classes
on the same matter, I made the assumption that in this case as well the minor mode
is formed with homogeneous ﬁelds, and taken away these ﬁelds from the class. All the
ﬁelds belonging to the minor modes for these classes have then been re-classed as ”Other
ﬁelds”.

4.3.2
Merging of classes
Distance between classes
The code used to derive the distance results shown here is presented appendix A.3.1.
Now that a subset of thirteen classes has been selected, and that their distributions have
been studied and the miss-labelled pixels have been re-assigned to the class ”Other ﬁelds”,
the next step is to merge together classes which are statistically very close to each other.
Some distances between classes can be calculated as a ﬁrst step to have an overview of
47
==PAGE:50==
the proximity of classes. Several distance measures exist to evaluate how separate two
distributions are. All of them are diﬀerent and may not highlight the same diﬀerences
between distributions, and therefore it is preferable to use several distance measures and
analyze all the results obtained to have a better idea of the diﬀerent classes. Two classes
consistently shown as very close to each other will then be more likely to be merged
together.
We recall that the PDF for two intensities R1 and R2 for a ﬁxed time point for a certain
class, as given in [19] and [18], is a bi-variate chi square distribution
P(R1, R2) =
nn+1(R1R2)
n−1
2
exp[−n
R1
C11
+ R2
C11
1−|ρc|2
]
(C11C22)
n+1
2 Γ(n)(1 −|ρc|2)|ρc|n−1 × In−1(2n
r
R1R2
C11C22
|ρc|
1 −|ρc|2)
(4.2)
where
– n is the ENL;
– C11 = E[R1];
– C22 = E[R2];
– |ρc| is the magnitude of the complex correlation coeﬃcient between R1 and R2 for
the given class;
– Γ is the gamma function;
– In is the modiﬁed Besseli function of order n.
The distribution for a class, for a given time point, is deﬁned by three parameters C11, C22
and their correlation magnitude |ρc|. Finally, to estimate the distances between classes
including all time points all together, we recall that the assumption has been made that
there is no correlation between observations for diﬀerent time points.
The ﬁrst distance measure tried on the classes is the Bhattacharyya distance. It is used in
classiﬁcation problems, to evaluate the proximity or the divergence between two statistical
samples. It is based a known probability density function (PDF) and can be used either
for continuous or discrete distributions.
For example, the Mahalanobis distance is a
special case of the Bhattacharyya distance for multi variate normal distributions. Since
the probability density function between the two intensities is known here, this distance
is interesting to know. Note that it is not a metric distance between two distributions,
but rather a divergence measure, as it does not verify the triangle inequality. The triangle
inequality is deﬁned as
D(p, r) ≤D(p, q) + D(q, r)
(4.3)
where p, q and r are the three mathematical objects on which we want to evaluate the
distance, and D(p, q) is the distance between p and q. The Bhattacharyya distance (or
48
==PAGE:51==
divergence) DB(p, q) between two probability distributions p and q for a random variable
x is deﬁned as
DB(p, q) = −log(
Z p
p(x)q(x)dx) for a continuous variable
DB(p, q) = −log(
X
x∈X
p
p(x)q(x)dx) for a discrete variable
(4.4)
Since the bi-variate chi square distribution has a complex PDF, the discrete variable form
of the Bhattacharyya divergence has been used to compute distances between classes. For
this purpose, a grid of the values of x = (R1, R2) on the whole interval has been used as
an approximation of the real divergence, which would be based on the integral form. This
method did not give very distinguishable results of divergence between classes, because the
approximation done on the grid is not accurate enough. This small inaccuracy appeared
in particular with the fact that the computed distance between a class and itself was non
zero. In order to account for this problem, the Bhattacharyya distance implemented in
this work has been slightly changed and can now be written
DB(p, q) = −log(
P
x∈X
p
p(x)q(x)dx
(P
x∈X p(x)dx)(P
x∈X q(x)dx)).
(4.5)
The divergence between the diﬀerent classes is presented in table 4.3. The distances are
symmetric. The numbers representing the classes in the distance table refer to the table
4.2. the study of table 4.3 shows several interesting patterns. First of all, the class 8
(Winter rape) is very diﬀerent from all other classes, and has a high distance with all of
them. Furthermore, some classes appear to be very close to each other. Classes 4 and 5
(Winter wheat 1 and winter wheat 2), 6 and 7 (Winter rye and winter hybrid rye) have
a distance inferior to one, and can be considered very close. Classes 9, 10, 11 and 12
(diﬀerent grass cover classes) also all have very small distances between them. However,
classes 10 and 12 are a bit more separated than they are with the others. Finally, it also
appears that classes 1 and 4 (spring barley and winter wheat 1) are rather close to each
other, with a Bhattacharyya distance of only two. All these classes close to each other
might need to be merged if they are too close statistically speaking.

I have thought and conceived another distance measure for this problem, adapted to its
speciﬁcity. It is based on the probability that the parameters describing a class belong
to the other class. As this probability is not symmetric between two classes, the distance
must be based on a mean of these two probabilities. I will give a more robust estimate
of the actual divergence between classes. Let’s denote ωi and ωj the two classes, and
xi and xj their respective intensity parameters (called later class centers, even if this
denomination is not mathematically exact).
That is, if for a given time point, ωi is
described by the parameters C11,i, C22,i and |ρc,i|, then xi = [C11,i , C22,i]. For the twelve
time points, this class center is simply the 24 intensity parameters describing the twelve
bi-variate chi-square distributions over the twelve time points. This ﬁrst coeﬃcient of
distance can be written
DC,1 = P(ωi|xj) + P(ωj|xi)
2
.
(4.6)
The distributions have very diﬀerent dispersions, and the value of this coeﬃcient depends
heavily on their dispersion. For example, a distribution with a small variance will auto-
matically give a low value to the coeﬃcient, as soon as the center of the other class is
not close to its own. The best way to account for this problem is to normalize DC,1 by
a measure of dispersion of classes. It has been chosen to divide it by the mean of the
probability that a center of a class belongs to its own class, for the two classes. Finally,
this measure is transformed as a divergence by taking the opposite of the logarithm of
the newly formed coeﬃcient. The distance measure is expressed as
DC(ωi, ωj) = −log(P(ωi|xj) + P(ωj|xi)
P(ωi|xi) + P(ωj|xj)).
(4.7)
The results of this distance measure, called here class center distance, are shown table 4.4.
As before, the classes numbers refer to the notation of classes introduced table 4.2. The
analysis of distances in table 4.4 reveals the same patterns as the ones found previously
with the Bhattacharyya distance table 4.3. The only diﬀerences is that classes 1 and 4
50
==PAGE:53==
do not appear as close to each other as for the other distance, and that now the distance
between classes 1 and 2 (spring barley and spring oats) is now fairly small.

Curves of average back scatter
The distances between classes can be visualized by looking at the curves presenting the
evolution of the average back scatter signals of all classes for all polarizations. If the
curves of two classes are consistently close to each other, for all polarizations, then the
two classes are very close to each other. These curves for the VV, VH polarizations and
their ratio are presented ﬁgures 4.10, 4.11 and 4.12 respectively. The distance patterns
found previously with tables 4.3 and 4.4 can be observed on these curves too. The curves
of the two winter wheat classes are very close to each other for both polarizations. The
same can be seen for the curves of the two winter rye classes, and for the four grass classes.
Moreover, it can be seen that the curves of spring barley and spring oats are also fairly
close to each other (as it was highlighted by the class center distance table 4.4). Finally,
the spring barley and winter wheat classes appear quite diﬀerent from these curves while
their Bhattacharyya distance given in table 4.3 is quite small. This could be due to a
high dispersion of the classes and therefore lead to an overlap of their distributions, which
cannot be seen by just looking at curves of average back scatters.

Furthermore, the curves of average back-scatters both in VV and VH polarizations (ﬁg-
ures 4.10 and 4.11) show for all classes important variations over time. All the curves
for all classes follow the same pattern with time: the values increase together for both
polarizations for the ﬁrst time point and from the 18/05 to the 17/07 and tend to be
lower for acquisitions in april and may. Such a behaviour is not expected, and the most
probable assumption which can explain it is changes in the weather for the acquisition.
Figure 4.13 shows the precipitations in mm for each acquisition date and the two previous
days. A very clear correlation can be seen between the rain curve and the patterns that
the average back scatter curves follow. This can be explained by the fact that a humid
ground has a diﬀerent dielectric constant, and tends to reﬂect a higher part of the power
sent by the SAR system. Acquisition days when the ground is dry will tend to reﬂect a
smaller back scatter intensity, and inversely for acquisition days with a wet ground.

Merging procedure
The distances between classes and a look at the curves of average back scatters give an
idea of how close or diﬀerent classes are. However, these measures alone are not enough
to determine if two classes are too close to each other statistically to be distinguishable
by a classiﬁer. The way used to determine which classes can be merged together was to
run the maximum likelihood classiﬁer presented subsection 3.1.4 on the data and study
the obtained confusion matrix after classiﬁcation. The used maximum likelihood classiﬁer
here is a pixel wise one, with the average ENL on all time points of 39.4. The classes
which show high degrees of confusion can be merged. The confusion matrix obtained after
classiﬁcation on all the classes in presented in table 4.5, and the corresponding classes are
presented in table 4.2. It appears from this ﬁrst confusion matrix that classes 4 and 5
(representing the classes Winter wheat 1 and Winter wheat 2), and 6 and 7 (representing
Winter rye and Winter hybrid rye) are very closely related, with a high confusion between
these classes couples. Moreover, it appears from the same confusion matrix that the grass
classes (classes 9, 10, 11 and 12) are also confused between themselves by the classiﬁer.
Note that all these classes with a high degree of confusion with the classiﬁer had very
small distances separating them in the previous part (tables 4.3 and 4.4). We can also
note that classes 1 and 2 (spring barley and spring oats) are also confused with each other
at a smaller degree. It also appears that classes 1 and 4 (spring barley and winter wheat
1), which were separated by a small Bhattacharyya distance are not specially confused a
lot by this classiﬁer.
Based on the results of the confusion matrix 4.5, and on the distances previously com-
puted, the following merges have been done on the data:
54
==PAGE:57==
– Classes 4 and 5 (winter wheat 1 and winter wheat 2)
– Classes 6 and 7 (winter rye and winter hybrid rye)
– Classes 9, 10, 11 and 12 (diﬀerent grass cover types).

do not show any sign of multi-modality. The same characteristics can be seen for the
histograms of all the other classes.

Finally, a box plot can also show how statistically the classes are related and how well
they can be separated. Box plots of the data present not only the average of the back
scatter intensity for a given polarization but also show its variance and show the degree
of dispersion of the data points for each class. Indeed, a box plot shows the average of a
distribution (the line in the middle of the box), and the limits of the box are the ﬁrst and
third quartiles of the said distribution. Finally, the end of the whiskers represent here the
ﬁrst and ninth deciles of the distribution, meaning that 80 % of the data are contained
within the two whiskers, and 50 % of the data within the box. It gives a very good
impression of how distinguishable classes are. All the box plots are not presented here.
Figure 4.22 presents the box plots for all classes at the tenth time point, for the intensity
ratio V V/V H. This ratio and time point have been chosen because this combination
appears to be on ﬁgure 4.18 the one which provides one of the most distinguishable
classes. It appears however that even if slight diﬀerences can be observed between classes,
they still are very close to each other, with a rather important dispersion of the pixels. It
also shows that the diﬀerence in averages of this ratio which are observed in the curves
ﬁgure 4.18 is not enough to separate well classes because of a relatively high variance and
dispersion. In other words, even if the boxes are separated a bit, the high dispersion and
variance of the data which can be observed with the whiskers of boxes and the remaining
data points around the boxes show that a classiﬁcation is going to be a challenging task,
if only one time point was to be used.

4.4
Dual intensity maximum likelihood classiﬁcation
The ML classiﬁer implemented here is explained subsection 3.1.4. For these classiﬁcations,
the training and the test sets are of the same size in a class. They are selected by randomly
selecting half of the pixels belonging to a class, based on the knowledge brought by the
ground reference.
The code used to produce the pixel wise classiﬁcations is presented appendix A.3.1, and
the one used for the ﬁeld wise classiﬁcations is presented appendix A.3.1. Both these codes
need to be slightly modiﬁed to obtain the diﬀerent versions of the classiﬁers presented in
this section. Moreover, they need a distance function. The distance function of the pixel
wise classiﬁcation is presented appendix A.3.1.
4.4.1
Pixel-wise classiﬁcation
In this subsection is presented the result of the pixel-wise classiﬁcation of the Stenløse data
set, with the classes obtained immediately before. The classiﬁcation presented here has
only been used for crop ﬁeld classes, so that its accuracy can be compared to the ﬁeld-
62
==PAGE:65==
wise classiﬁcation results presented later. Moreover, the same ENL (of 39.4) has been
used for all the diﬀerent images in this classiﬁcation. The selected classes as presented
in table4.6. The obtained confusion matrix after classiﬁcation on the twelve images is
presented table4.7. This classiﬁcation is rather satisfactory, and the classes are diﬀerent
enough to be classed fairly well, as it can be seen from the confusion matrix.

pixels in the classiﬁcation. For a given pixel, the entropy is deﬁned as
H = −
K
X
i=1
Pi logK(Pi)
(4.8)
where K is the number of classes and Pi is the probability that the pixel belongs to the
ith class. The entropy value can theoretically vary between zero and one. An entropy
close to zero means that the certainty of the pixel’s classiﬁcation is high, while a high
entropy means that this certainty is low. Finally, the class represented in black in the
classiﬁcation is a reject class. The reject class is composed of pixels which entropy value

is over a threshold set at 0.8, over which the trust that one can put in the classiﬁcation is

and the class, and not the entropy measure. The goal of the study is to compare the
results obtained with several classiﬁers, including some which are not based on the data’s
distribution and for which a distance measure cannot be found (such as the random forest
classiﬁer). It is therefore a good idea to use a measure which can be extracted from all
classiﬁers.

Moreover, it is a good idea to study the user and the producer accuracies evolution in
function of the number of time points for each class, and to compare them with the
results obtained with the classiﬁcation based only on a single time point. Such studies
65
==PAGE:68==
are presented ﬁgure 4.27. For all classes but the grass one, the main tendency of both
accuracies is to go up with an increasing number of time points. It appears that the
producer accuracy of the grass class doesn’t increase with the number of images available,
and its user accuracy increases only slowly. However, any accuracy beneﬁts a lot from the
increasing number of time points. Moreover, it appears that some time points are better
than others to diﬀerentiate a speciﬁc class from the others, bringing valuable information
which is kept by the classiﬁer using all of the time points. Finally, an interesting thing
to note is that the producer accuracy for spring barley for the single time point (in blue)
has a peak at the ninth time point, which is the second week of June. It corresponds to
the ﬂowering of the crop. The inverse phenomenon is observed for the single time point
producer accuracy for winter barley which decreases suddenly at the end of may. This
also coincides with winter barley’s ﬂowering, which apparently makes it more diﬃcult to
distinguish from other classes. However, the producer accuracy peak for winter rye on
the ﬁfth time point, which is the end of april doesn’t coincide with any special stage of
development of the crop.

Finally, the evolution of the average producer accuracy for the classiﬁer in function of
the number of time points is presented ﬁgure 4.28. The average producer accuracy is
a good measure of the classiﬁcation’s accuracy. Even if the user accuracy might seem
more interesting, since it represents after classiﬁcation for a class the portion of pixels
really belonging to the said class. It is however very dependent on the class size, and
a small class can have a very low user accuracy even if the classiﬁcation is good. The
inverse eﬀect appears for the number of pixels well classed: it gives a higher weight to
the biggest classes, and small classes with a bad classiﬁcation accuracy would not appear
there. Finally, the producer accuracy for a class doesn’t depend on its size compared
to the others, and taking the average of the producer accuracies therefore gives a good
idea of the classiﬁer’s capability to class accurately. For all but the last one, this average
accuracy shows a great improvement for each additional time point. This could be due to a
harvesting happening between the eleventh and the twelfth image. The average producer
accuracy goes as high as 75%, which is still below the usual accuracy request asked by
ESA, which is of 85%. However, the water, forest and urban area classes, when added, will
show a way higher accuracy than the crop ﬁelds classes, which are more diﬃcult to tell
apart, and the addition of these classes would surely improve a lot the average producer
accuracy shown here.

In this section, the pixel wise maximum likelihood classiﬁcation of the data is also studied.

the ENL is not assumed to be the same for all time points, but that each time point has
its own ENL. Theoretically, the ENL of an instrument is not supposed to vary over time,

and the best way to estimate the ENL is to take the mean of several ENLs on several

results are extremely similar to the ones obtained with the classiﬁer with a global ENL,
but this classiﬁer performs a bit worse in average, because of a small drop in the average
user accuracy. The rest of the results is not presented in this report, as they are almost
similar as the ones obtained for the previous classiﬁer.
4.4.3
Field-wise classiﬁcation
This subsection describes the results obtained on the ﬁeld wise classiﬁcation, still using
the dual intensity maximum likelihood classiﬁer. For the classiﬁcation, each ﬁeld is rep-
resented by the average of its pixels, and this average on both intensities is then classed.
The training data used is directly provided by the pixels, and not by the ﬁeld data. The
number of ﬁelds in the classes is indeed too small to provide good statistical distributions
for training the data on ﬁeld attributes. The obtained confusion matrix is presented ta-
ble 4.9. As before, the classes in the classiﬁcation are listed table 4.6. All the diﬀerent
measures of accuracy for all classes have signiﬁcantly improved compared to the pixel wise
classiﬁcation. This comes from the very high noise speckle reduction done when averaging
all the pixels in one ﬁeld, since this noise is present at a pixel level. In this classiﬁcation,
the ENL has been treated as a constant over the whole image and for all time points,
but it could be used more locally for each ﬁeld. Such a classiﬁcation is presented later,
subsection 4.4.4. Moreover, the transformation to the ﬁeld wise classiﬁcation reduces the
variance of observations for each class, because of the noise reduction, which makes the
classes more distinct. However, this kind of classiﬁcation requires either to have a previous
knowledge of the study zone, or to use a powerful edge detection to separate homogeneous
regions beforehand.
The resulting classiﬁcation is only obtained on ﬁelds, and is presented ﬁgure 4.29. The
ground reference is presented again ﬁgure 4.30 for visual comparison. The Shannon en-

to the pixel wise one, and the entropy increases a bit for two classes, while staying very
similar overall. Therefore, for the crop classiﬁcation, the ﬁeld wise classiﬁcation seems
more promising than the simple pixel wise one.

4.4.4
Field-wise classiﬁcation, with ﬁeld wise ENL
The classiﬁcation studied in this section is still obtained with the dual intensity maximum
likelihood classiﬁer. This time again, the classiﬁcation is ﬁeld wise, and based on training
sets obtained on pixel information. Each ﬁeld is represented by its average pixel, which
is then classed. However, as it explained in subsection 3.1.4, the maximum likelihood
classiﬁer uses the ENL information. The idea here was to evaluate if using for each ﬁeld
and time its particular ENL can bring improvement to the classiﬁcation. The confusion
matrix of the obtained classiﬁcation is presented table 4.10. Overall, compared to the
ﬁeld wise classiﬁcation using the same ENL for all images, the classiﬁcation accuracy has
decreased. The producer accuracy has decreased for ﬁve classes out of seven. The only
classes with a better producer accuracy are winter rye, which accuracy stays stable, and
grass, which producer accuracy has increased of 0.9 %. It appears that using a ﬁeld wise
ENL for classiﬁcation gives worse results than using a general ENL.
Furthermore, the other results obtained with this classiﬁcation compared to the previous
ﬁeld wise classiﬁcation show the same trend: they are very similar and always a bit worse.
The entropy of classiﬁcation is a bit increased in average, and the evolution of the average
producer accuracy over the number of images shows the same pattern but always giving
a bit worse accuracy.

4.5
Conclusion on the Sentinel-1 data set classiﬁca-
tion
The accuracy improvement of maximum likelihood classiﬁers with multidate data has
been assessed on the Stenløse data set acquired with Sentinel-1. The improvement has
been studied both class per class and for the total classiﬁcation accuracy. It has been
chosen to use the average producer accuracy as a measure of accuracy for the classiﬁer,
as it does not depend on the relative size of the diﬀerent classes. The accuracy increase
is less and less signiﬁcant as the number of acquisition dates increase, as it has been
observed ﬁgures 4.28 and 4.36. Furthermore, speciﬁc dates during the growing season
corresponding to changes of the crops such as ﬂowering can help to greatly improve the
classiﬁcation accuracy for this crop class.
In addition, a comparison of the classiﬁcation accuracy has been made between diﬀerent
classiﬁers. Pixel-wise classiﬁcation has proven to be less reliable than ﬁeld wise classi-
ﬁcation. However, a previous knowledge of the area is needed to perform a ﬁeld wise
classiﬁcation, since it requires to know the boundaries of the diﬀerent ﬁelds. It has also
been found that pixel wise classiﬁcation with an acquisition-wise ENL does not perform
better than the classiﬁer using the average ENL for all acquisitions. Finally, using the
ﬁeld-wise ENL in ﬁeld wise classiﬁcation actually results in a worse accuracy than the
normal ﬁeld-wise classiﬁcation with an average ENL for all acquisitions and all pixels.
However, this study still has a weakness, which is that ﬁeld-wise classiﬁcation has not
been performed on a classiﬁer trained with ﬁeld data, but trained with pixel data, as the
number of ﬁelds in the studied classes were too small to provide a statistically acceptable
training set.
The impact of weather conditions on data acquisition has also been observed: wet ground
cover tends to increase the back scatter power.
76
==PAGE:79==
Chapter 5
Classiﬁcation on the Foulum dataset
5.1
The data set
The data set studied in this chapter has been obtained over the Foulum area. It is a
research area for the Danish Institute of Agricultural Sciences and is used as a test area.
It contains a large variety of crops and natural areas, such as forests and lakes. The data
studied here consists in one set of fully polarimetric SAR measurements taken over this
area, in L-band (1.25 GHz/24 cm wavelength). It has been acquired on May the 20th.
The data has been speckle ﬁltered, using the Lee ﬁlter, explained in length in [16]. The
ENL found after speckle ﬁltering of the data is 25. The ENL has been estimated using the
technique explained in [3]. Pre-processing of data is not the main subject here, and are
therefore the ENL estimation and the speckle ﬁltering used are not further documented.
A study of the classiﬁcation accuracy improvement over time and with several acquisition
has already been done with the Sentinel 1 data set in chapter 4. The goal of the study
over this area is double. The ﬁrst objective is to compare the quality of classiﬁcation
obtained with fully polarimetric data and some of its subsets (dual polarimetric data, with
only intensity or not, and single polarization). The second objective is to compare the
classiﬁcation results and the information obtained based on the diﬀerent decompositions
presented chapter 2.
An optical image of the data set is presented ﬁgure 5.1.
77
==PAGE:80==
Figure 5.1: Aerial image of the area studied.
5.2
Selection of classes
The code used to produce the results shown in this section is presented appendix A.3.2.
The area is used for research purposes, therefore the crops growing there are well refer-
enced there is some ground reference elements available for the studied area for some crop
ﬁelds. Moreover, there is a lake and two diﬀerent kinds of forest cover. An image of the
|HV |2 power is presented ﬁgure 5.2. It is already possible from this image to diﬀerentiate
some ﬁelds, and to see the lake and the two diﬀerent kinds of forest cover. An image of
the ground reference with the diﬀerent ﬁelds is presented ﬁgure 5.3. Forest and water
have already been added to this ground reference. A single class of forest is taken here
to represent the two diﬀerent kinds of forest cover. This stage is indeed supposed to be
followed by a study of the classes’ distributions, and a division of classes presenting multi-
modal distributions in sub-classes. It is in this stage that the two diﬀerent kinds of forest
will be diﬀerentiated. The type of crop corresponding to each used color is referenced
table 5.1.

As written above, the next step is to study the histograms of all classes for all attributes
to see whether some of them present multi-modal distributions, and if they do, subdivide
them in sub-classes. For this subdivision of classes, the only attributes used have been
the three polarization intensities in dB (the three diagonal elements of the covariance
matrix put in dB). These three elements carry most of the statistical information, and
can provide a good subset to make a selection of classes. While they could also be used
for this purpose, the attributes obtained from the diﬀerent decompositions applied to
the data set and the non diagonal elements of the covariance matrix have not been used
here, because it would amount to a very important number of diﬀerent class selections
to compare each time with several classiﬁers, and led to a report way too long. The
histograms of the distribution of data in these ten classes are presented ﬁgure 5.4 for HH
intensity, ﬁgure 5.5 for HV intensity and ﬁgure 5.6 for VV intensity.

It can be seen from these histograms that several classes present multi-modal distributions,
and need to be subdivided. Some of them even present multi-modal distributions in several
polarimetric intensities. The problem has been handled in the same way as for the Sentinel
1 data set: for a class with a multi-modal distribution for a certain attribute, a threshold
81
==PAGE:84==
is manually selected to separate the diﬀerent distributions at best, and the corresponding
splits are plotted to see if it corresponds to homogeneous ﬁelds diﬀerent from the others
or if it is a within class variation. If for a same class, several attributes show a multi-
modal distribution, the intensity showing the best separation of the sub-classes is chosen
to decompose the class. Finally, things were handled a bit diﬀerently for the forest class,
which did not show any multi-modal behaviour, but an important tail in the distribution
for each intensity. To extract the subclass from the forest class, only the pixels belonging
to the three tails simultaneously have been selected. As it will be seen later, this approach
has been successful. In a ﬁrst time, the other classes subdivided are
– Grass class using the VV intensity;
– Winter wheat using the HH intensity;
– Spring barley using the HV intensity;
– Peas using the HV intensity;
– Winter barley using the HH intensity.
The splits done on these classes can be visualized ﬁgure 5.7. For each class, the corre-
sponding ﬁgure shows only the pixels belonging to the class, and the others in blue. The
pixels belonging to the main mode of the histogram are in yellow, and the ones in the
smaller mode in red. It can be seen that these subdivisions always end up in separating
homogeneous ﬁelds from the main class.
82
==PAGE:85==
Grass

ﬁgures 5.8, 5.9 and 5.10 for respectively HH, HV and VV intensities in dB. It can be

behaviour. Some classes now contain a fairly small amount of pixels (such as the beets or
the second grass classes) and their histograms can therefore sometimes seem a bit weird
