5.3
Diﬀerent decompositions
Since the Foulum data set is fully polarimetric, it is possible to apply to it all the decom-
positions explained chapter 2, and use the obtained parameters as attributes for classiﬁ-
cation, and compare how much information each decomposition can summarize. All the
decompositions and the results shown in this section are obtained with the code presented
appendix A.3.2.
5.3.1
Entropy-α-anisotropy decomposition
Fully polarimetric case
This decomposition, explained section 2.3, has been applied to the data set. Figure 5.11
presents the resulting map of Cloude entropy, ﬁgure 5.12 shows the map of the α angle
and ﬁgure 5.13 shows the map of anisotropy. In all these ﬁgures, one can easily see some
diﬀerent types of ﬁelds or ground cover. However, there is still some ﬂuctuation of all of
these attributes on homogeneous areas, meaning that it could make classiﬁcation based
on them a bit uncertain.
Moreover, the classiﬁcation scheme proposed by Cloude and Pottier directly on the values
of the entropy and α (which map is shown ﬁgure 2.2) can be quickly compared to an
actual classiﬁcation of the ground cover. A full Wishart classiﬁcation has been made on
the data set and is presented later (the resulting classiﬁcation can be seen ﬁgure 5.24a,
and its average producer accuracy is of 80.3 %). The data has been decomposed using the
85
==PAGE:88==
Cloude and Pottier method, and plotted on the Entropy-α plane, and each pixel has been
coloured with the colour of the class it has been classed as in the Wishart classiﬁcation.
The resulting entropy-α plane is shown ﬁgure 5.14. The colours represent the classes as
it is planned table 5.1. The results coincide with the expected physical properties of the
classes: for example water (in blue) is in a zone with a low entropy and a low α angle,
which means that it is mainly explained by surface scattering, and that the entropy is
low. The classes are also regrouped together. However, they do not coincide well with
the boxes derived by Cloude and Pottier ﬁgure 2.2.

Dual polarimetric case
The dual polarimetric case has also been studied, for the HV-VV polarization.
The
two methods for decomposing dual polarimetric data presented subsection 2.3.2 appeared
to give exactly the same results. As for the fully polarimetric case, the decomposition
is shown on the entropy-α plane with the colours corresponding to the same Wishart
classiﬁcation as before ﬁgure 5.15. Compared to the fully polarimetric case, the data has
shifted to the bottom of the plane, and is compressed in a smaller interval of the α angle.
The classes are now quite mixed together, at the exception of the water (in blue), the rye
(in red) and the forest (in green) classes.
Figure 5.15: Pixels and their classiﬁcation on the entropy-α plane.
88
==PAGE:91==
5.3.2
Comparison of the other decompositions
All the other decompositions explained chapter 3 give as a result of the decomposition
very similar parameters. The even number of bounces scattering given by the Van Zyl and
NNED decompositions is the same as the double bounce scattering, and the odd number
of bounces of these decompositions is equivalent to the surface scattering of the others.
All of their results can therefore be compared directly.
Figure 5.16 represents the fraction the total scattering power explained by volume scat-
tering in percentage for all of the four decompositions on a map of the area, in shades
of green. The drawback of the Freeman and Durden and the Yamaguchi decompositions
explained by Van Zyl in [29], that these decompositions can lead to negative powers ap-
pears very clearly here. The color bar from ﬁgure 5.16a goes to 110 %, and the one from
ﬁgure 5.16c goes to 150 %, meaning that on these pixels, the other scattering powers have
to be negative. However, the distribution of the volume scattering power is the same
on all decompositions: the same ﬁelds have low volume scattering contribution, and the
same areas have strong contributions. It also appears than the Van Zyl decomposition,
ﬁgure 5.16b has shifted values of volume scattering importance compared to the other
decompositions, as its maximum goes up to a 30 % participation, as it can be seen from
the boundaries of its color bar.

It can be seen easily from these three comparisons that the decompositions give similar
distributions for all powers for the same areas, but these distributions can be shifted a bit.
Generally speaking, a pixel with a certain importance of a speciﬁc scattering mechanism
in one decomposition will have the same relative importance in the other decompositions.
A closer look to these distributions can be seen by looking directly at the histograms of
the power explained by these mechanisms for each decomposition and for the whole data
set. These histograms are presented appendix A.2. Figure A.4 presents these histograms
for the volume scattering power, ﬁgure A.5 for the surface scattering power and ﬁgure A.6
for the double bounce scattering. A look to these histograms show that the distributions
obtained from the diﬀerent decompositions are indeed very similar. They should therefore
give similar results if used as attributes in a classiﬁer.
5.3.3
Canopy choice comparison
Another comparison can be made between the NNED and the Yamaguchi decompositions,
on their canopy choice. For both decompositions, the same three diﬀerent canopy models
have been used: one for a canopy oriented vertically, one for a canopy oriented horizontally
and one for a canopy randomly oriented. The maps of the chosen canopy model for each
pixel for these two decompositions can be seen ﬁgure 5.19. In these maps, red pixels
represent a vertically oriented canopy, green ones a horizontally oriented canopy and blue
91
==PAGE:94==
ones a randomly oriented canopy. The canopy choice in the Yamaguchi decomposition is
pretty diverse, with mainly randomly oriented canopy for ﬁelds, a vertically oriented one
for the forest, and some ﬁelds are in green. The canopy model in the NNED decomposition
is very diﬀerent: most of the ﬁelds are in red with a vertical canopy, and some of the ﬁelds
still have a randomly oriented one. There are almost no green pixels, so no horizontally
oriented canopy.
Chosen canopy model with the Yamaguchi decomposition
(a) Yamaguchi decomposition
Chosen canopy model with the NNED decomposition
(b) NNED decomposition
Figure 5.19: Map of the chosen canopy model. Red pixels represent a vertically oriented
canopy model, green ones a horizontally oriented one and blue pixel a randomly oriented
canopy.
5.4
Maximum likelihood classiﬁer
The maximum likelihood classiﬁers, presented section 3.1, have been implemented and
used on the Foulum data set. Since this data set is fully polarimetric, the full Wishart
classiﬁer can be used to class the data, and any subset of the covariance matrix can be
artiﬁcially selected and used for classiﬁcation. The idea here is to use all the diﬀerent
maximum likelihood classiﬁers presented section 3.1 on all the possible polarization com-
binations, and compare their accuracy on the classes. The second goal of this section is
to assess the importance of the selection of classes step for maximum likelihood classiﬁca-
tion. How important is it to divide classes with multi-modal behaviours for an attribute
in sub-classes? This result can afterwards be used as a base to compare the relative im-
portance of this step for the random forest classiﬁer. In order to compare the results from
the two conﬁgurations (10 diﬀerent classes before the subdivision step and 20 after), once
the classiﬁcation has been done on the 20 classes, the diﬀerent sub-classes obtained from
a same class are merged together again. Therefore, all the results are presented on the
ten classes presented table 5.1.
In both cases, the training and test sets are selected in the same way. For each class, the
training set is created by randomly selecting half of the pixels of the class in the ground
reference. The test set for the class is composed of the other half of the pixels.
No merging procedure has been implemented for this data set, since merging consists in
bringing together classes which are too close statistically to be diﬀerentiated by a classiﬁer.
Since here several diﬀerent classiﬁers are used on the data, the merging procedures would
92
==PAGE:95==
also need to be diﬀerent for each classiﬁer. This way, one can also compare how well
diﬀerent classiﬁers can distinguish between relatively close classes. All the results shown
in this section and all the maximum likelihood classiﬁers presented here are implemented
in the code presented in appendix A.3.2.
5.4.1
Maximum likelihood classiﬁcation on the 10 basic classes
The classiﬁcations presented here are made with classiﬁers trained only on the ﬁrst ten
classes, without using the subdivisions made on the classes and explained section 5.2.
The classes’ numbers in the diﬀerent tables presented here use the notation of the classes
introduced table 5.1. In order to visualize better the selection of pixels for the training sets
of the classiﬁers, the training sets used here are shown ﬁgure 5.20b, next to the ground
reference, ﬁgure 5.20a.

Figure 5.20: Comparison of the ground reference and the training sets of the data.
Table 5.2 gives a summary of the producer accuracy for each class and each diﬀerent
classiﬁer, along with the average producer accuracy. Moreover, ﬁgure 5.21 presents these
results in another way to visualize them better.
Figure 5.21a presents the producer
accuracy for the diﬀerent classes for all the diﬀerent single intensity classiﬁers, ﬁgure
5.21b is the equivalent for dual intensity classiﬁers, and ﬁgure 5.21c presents the same for
reduced dual polarimetric Wishart classiﬁers. Finally, ﬁgure 5.21d presents the diﬀerent
producer accuracies for the HV-VV dual intensity classiﬁer and the dual polarimetric
reduced Wishart classiﬁer, along with the full Wishart classiﬁer. This result is interesting
since it gives more insight about the HV-VV dual polarimetric case, which is the one
studied earlier for the Sentinel 1 data. This ﬁgure shows well the kind of improvement
which could be brought if the data was not consisting only in two intensities, but would
also incorporate the two oﬀ-diagonal elements of the covariance matrix (giving the reduced
Wishart classiﬁer), and how these classiﬁers compare to the full Wishart one.
Several important results can be observed both from the table and the ﬁgures. First of all,
and for all the classiﬁers, the producer accuracy for class three (winter wheat is extremely
low). Secondly, only the single intensity classiﬁer for VV polarization, the reduced Wishart
classiﬁer with HH-VV polarizations and the full Wishart classiﬁer manage to class well
class seven (spring oats). This is especially interesting since it means that the oﬀ-diagonal
93
==PAGE:96==
elements of the covariance matrix are especially important to diﬀerentiate this class from
the others, as the HH-VV dual intensity classiﬁer also has a very low producer accuracy
for this class.
A look at ﬁgure 5.21a shows what kind of classes each polarization is good at diﬀerentiating
from the others. For example, the HV polarization is really good for diﬀerentiating grass,
or the HH one performs very well on beets. In the same way, we can see which classes are
diﬃcult to diﬀerentiate for them. For example, all the single polarizations perform badly
for peas.
Moreover, it appears that for the dual intensity classiﬁers, the HH-VV combination is
performing worse than the others in terms of average producer accuracy as it can be seen
table 5.2. However, still by looking at the table, this polarization combination performs
way better than the others for the reduced Wishart classiﬁer, in terms of average producer
accuracy. It can also be observed from ﬁgure 5.21c that the HH-VV classiﬁer consistently
has a better producer accuracy than the others for almost all classes. This highlights the
high importance of the oﬀ-diagonal elements of the covariance matrix for the HH-VV dual
polarization.
Except from the HH-VV dual polarization, there is no really an improvement in terms of
average producer accuracy by adding the oﬀ-diagonal elements of the covariance matrix
to the classiﬁer: the HH-HV dual intensity classiﬁers even performs a bit better than the
reduced Wishart one on the same polarizations. This can be observed ﬁgure 5.21d, for
the HV-VV dual polarization. It can be seen there that the reduced Wishart classiﬁer
performs better than the dual intensity one for spring oats, but worse for beets, and is
more or less equivalent to it for other classes. It can also be seen that as expected, the
full Wishart classiﬁer performs better than the others for all classes, and has a very high
average producer accuracy.

5.4.2
Maximum likelihood classiﬁcation on all the 20 classes
In this section is studied the classiﬁcation obtained with classiﬁers trained on the 20
classes obtained at the subdivision step. Table 5.3 presents the producer accuracy for
each class for each classiﬁer, and the average producer accuracy for each classiﬁer. The
same plots as before but for these classiﬁers are presented ﬁgure 5.23. Furthermore, ﬁgure
5.22 presents the comparison between the ground reference (containing the 20 classes) and
their corresponding training sets.

5.4.3
Comparison of classiﬁcation with and without subdivision.
The purpose of this subsection is to compare the results obtained between the classiﬁcation
with the classiﬁers trained on ten classes and the one obtained with the classiﬁers trained
on 20 classes. Several important observations can be made when comparing the results
in tables 5.2 and 5.3.
– It appears that the average producer accuracy of single polarization classiﬁers is
better when they are trained with ten classes than 20.
– Still in terms of average producer accuracy, all the other classiﬁers perform better
when trained on 20 classes than ten classes. The progression is very small for dual
intensity classiﬁers, better for reduced Wishart ones, and even better for the full
99
==PAGE:102==
Wishart one (going from an average producer accuracy of 80.3 % when trained on
ten classes to 89.9 % when trained on the 20 classes).
– The classiﬁcation of winter wheat (class three) is way better for the classiﬁers trained
on 20 classes.
– In the same way and as expected, the classiﬁers trained on 20 classes also have a
higher producer accuracy for the forest class for most them, compared to the ones
trained on ten classes.
– The classiﬁcation of beets is worse for classiﬁers trained on 20 classes than ten,
except for the HH-VV reduced Wishart classiﬁer and the full Wishart classiﬁer.
– The producer accuracy for peas is worse for the HV-VV dual polarization when
trained on 20 classes than ten (both for the dual intensity and the reduced Wishart
classiﬁers), but way better for the two other dual polarizations.
– The full Wishart classiﬁer trained on 20 classes has a better producer accuracy for
every class than the one trained only on ten classes.
This evolution in average producer accuracy performance shows that the division of classes
in sub-classes is beneﬁcial only when the classiﬁer can diﬀerentiate these sub-classes well.
This highlights the importance of an adequate merging procedure for every diﬀerent clas-
siﬁer, based on the distance between classes for this particular classiﬁer.
The single
polarimetric classiﬁers have indeed a degraded overall performance when trained on 20
classes because they are not able to diﬀerentiate well all the classes created at the subdi-
vision step. For classiﬁers with more attributes, such as the full Wishart one, which is till
able to diﬀerentiate all the classes well, this step was extremely beneﬁcial, and allowed it
to perform way better on the split classes, such as winter wheat. The confusion matrices
obtained with the two full Wishart classiﬁers are presented table 5.4 for the one trained
on ten classes, and table 5.5 for the one trained on 20 classes.
The two obtained classiﬁcations are shown ﬁgure 5.24. It can be seen by comparing these
two classiﬁcations that an important fraction of the pixels classed as winter barley in violet
by the classiﬁer trained on ten classes is classed diﬀerently, and for a great part of it as
winter wheat in yellow. This result can also be observed by comparing the two confusion
matrices. It can indeed be seen table 5.4 (the classiﬁcation trained on ten classes) that
winter wheat pixels are mainly classed as winter barley. The improvement observed on
the two classiﬁcations can be assessed by the huge improvement of the user accuracy for
winter barley, which goes from 12.4 % to 43.2 %.

5.5
Random forest classiﬁcation
Several diﬀerent random forest classiﬁers have been trained diﬀerently on the Foulum data
set, and used to class the data afterwards. There are two diﬀerent objectives to study in
this section. Firstly, it is interesting to use the random forest classiﬁer on the elements of
the covariance matrix and compare the resulting classiﬁcation accuracy for the classiﬁer
trained on ten classes and the one trained on the 20 classes obtained at the subdivision
of classes step. This will provide information on whether this pre-processing of classes
is important for a random forest classiﬁer. This can be compared to the result obtained
immediately above showing that this step was important for maximum likelihood classi-
ﬁers. Secondly, the other point of the section is to compare the classiﬁcations obtained
when trained on the diﬀerent attributes obtained with all the diﬀerent decompositions
presented chapter 2.
As explained section 3.2, the training sets provided to a random forest classiﬁer need to
be of roughly the same size, so that the miss-classiﬁcation of smaller classes are not dis-
regarded or weighted less than miss-classiﬁcation on the bigger ones. Another processing
step therefore needs to be added before training the random forest classiﬁer on a data
set. The pixels of the ground reference are ﬁrst divided in training and test sets of the
same size, as for the maximum likelihood classiﬁcation. Afterwards, a number of wanted
observations is manually chosen, between the maximum and minimum number of pixels
in the training sets of all classes. The training sets are modiﬁed so that they all have more
or less the same size. This can be done by oversampling (that is increasing the size of
the training set by replicating some of the observations) if the training set is smaller than
the wanted size of training set. The other way around, where only a random subset of
the observations is selected, is called undersampling, and is used for training sets bigger
than the wanted size. The handling of the training set size between the diﬀerent classes
is done with the code presented appendix A.3.2.
The random forest classiﬁer used here is the one already implemented in matlab, called
TreeBagger. The code used for random forest classiﬁcation is presented appendix A.3.2.
Inner cross validation loops are already implemented in it, to optimize the diﬀerent pa-
102
==PAGE:105==
rameters of the classiﬁcation, such as the maximum depth of trees. For each classiﬁcation
training, an outer cross validation loop has been used to estimate the optimal number of
trees needed to minimize the miss-classiﬁcation, but keep the number of trees at a reason-
able level. Figure 5.25 presents the error rate curve in function of the number of trees for
the random forest classiﬁer trained on the Freeman and Durden decomposition parame-
ters. It can be observed that the error rate decreases fast at ﬁrst, and has an asymptotic
behaviour afterwards. In this case, a number of trees of 30 is enough to ensure a minimum
generalization error for example.

Figure 5.25: Classiﬁcation error rate in function of the number of trees in the random
forest classiﬁer trained on the components of the Freeman and Durden decomposition.
Table 5.6 presents for each classiﬁer, its producer accuracy on each class and its average
producer accuracy. As explained before, the average producer accuracy is chosen as the
measure of accuracy of a classiﬁer, as it does not depend on the size of the diﬀerent classes.
The classes’ numbers in the table refer to the numbering of classes introduced table 5.1.
In the subdivision of classes step, the classes subdivided were
– Grass (class 2);
– Winter wheat (class 3);
– Spring barley (class 4);
– Peas (class 5);
– Winter barley (class 6);
– Forest (class 9).
103
==PAGE:106==
By looking at the ﬁrst two lines of table 5.6, it is possible to compare the producer accuracy
of the diﬀerent classes for the random forest classiﬁers trained on the full covariance
matrix, on either 20 or ten classes. It appears that the producer accuracy varies slightly
from class to class, without apparent reason. The classiﬁer trained on 20 classes does
not perform better than the one trained on ten for all of the subdivided classes, but only
a fraction of them. Overall, the two classiﬁer have almost the same average producer
accuracy (96.3 % against 96.6%). This shows that the subdivision of classes showing
multi-modal behaviours for some attributes in smaller sub-classes is not necessary for
a random forest classiﬁer, and does not inﬂuence its accuracy.
Indeed, the producer
accuracy ﬂuctuation between the two classiﬁers for a same class is very likely to be due
to the diﬀerence in the two trained classiﬁers, since the overall accuracy is the same.
Since the training method of a random forest classiﬁer is randomized, it means that
two classiﬁers trained on the same data can give slightly diﬀerent classiﬁers (and thus
classiﬁcation results), but leading to the same overall accuracy.
The classiﬁers trained on the attributes obtained through the diﬀerent decompositions
have all been trained on the ten classes presented table 5.1, since it has just been shown
that subdividing classes does not impact a random tree classiﬁer. Moreover, ﬁgure 5.26
presents in a more visual way the results presented table 5.6 for the classiﬁers trained on
the diﬀerent decompositions. It can be seen that the Cloude and Pottier decomposition
(the entropy-α-anisotropy decomposition) gives a worse classiﬁcation than the other de-
compositions. The classiﬁers trained on the other decompositions all have very similar
producer accuracies for all classes, and have almost the same average producer accuracy.
Finally, two other combinations have been tried and are shown only in table 5.6. As
suggested by Alberga in [2], the total power has been added to the entropy-α-anisotropy
decomposition, to see if it improves the classiﬁcation. It appears that it does improve
it a lot, as the average producer accuracy of the classiﬁers jump from 78.5 % for the
simple decomposition to 91.2 % for the decomposition with the total power. In the same
way, a classiﬁer trained on the attributes obtained with both the entropy-α-anisotropy
and Freeman and Durden decompositions has been tried. It also greatly improved the
classiﬁcation compared to the ones obtained with only one of the two decompositions,
since its average producer accuracy is of 92.4 %. It also performs slightly better than the
classiﬁer trained on entropy-α-anisotropy decomposition with the total power.

5.6
Conclusion on the Foulum data set classiﬁcation
The study made on the Foulum data set has lead to several interesting results. It has
appeared that for maximum likelihood classiﬁers, the oﬀ-diagonal elements for the HH-
VV polarization are very important for classiﬁcation, when the other oﬀ-diagonal elements
have a small importance. This observation is in accordance with what can be seen from
most of the decompositions explained chapter 2. Indeed, in most of these decompositions,
the assumption of azimuthal symmetry was made, meaning that a covariance matrix can
be expressed
[C] = K


1
0
ρ
0
η
0
ρ∗
0
ζ

.
(5.1)
Here, the other oﬀ-diagonal elements than the HH-VV ones are not present, and do not
play an important role in classiﬁcation. Furthermore, while subdividing classes presenting
multi-modal behaviours for at least one attribute is crucial to improve maximum likelihood
classiﬁcation, this step is unnecessary for a random forest classiﬁer. Merging statistically
close classes is however important, and this step must be done diﬀerently for each classiﬁer
and each diﬀerent set of attributes.
Finally, random forest classiﬁers have been used
on the attributes obtained from the diﬀerent decompositions presented in this work, to
compare how well they summarize information contained in their attributes. It appears
that the entropy-α-anisotropy decomposition gives a worst classiﬁcation than the other
decompositions, which all give very similar results. When adding the total power to this
decomposition though, it becomes the best performing one.
Another study which would have been interesting to do here but has not been done would
have been to train a random forest classiﬁer only on the ﬁve elements of the covariance
matrix in the case of azimuthal symmetry, and compare the accuracy of the obtained
classiﬁcation to the one obtained with the full covariance matrix.
106
==PAGE:109==
Chapter 6
Conclusion
Two diﬀerent studies have been made on ground cover classiﬁcation based on SAR ac-
quired data on two diﬀerent data sets. An important number of diﬀerent ways to classify
ground cover have been compared and assessed.
On the Sentinel-1 data set, the focus of the study has been on the study of the evolution
of the classiﬁcation accuracy on a variety of crop ﬁelds with the number of acquisitions
during the growing season. This data set was composed of acquisitions on twelve times
points, of two intensities, for HV-VV polarization. An extensive study has been done
on the diﬀerent classes to keep in the data set. A subdivision of classes has then been
carried out on the selected classes, to have unimodal distributions for all features for all
classes. Afterwards, a distance has been computed between the diﬀerent classes, and some
of them, judged too statistically too close to each other for the classiﬁers to diﬀerentiate
them have been merged. It has appeared that all crop classes, except the grass ﬁelds,
greatly beneﬁt from the increase of acquisition points. However, the more time points are
already available, the less the addition of a new acquisition at a new time point beneﬁts
the classiﬁcation accuracy, until an almost asymptotic behaviour of the accuracy evolution
can be observed. A closer look at the evolution of the producer accuracy for each class in
function of the number of time points show that special events in the crop development
such as ﬂowering can inﬂuence it greatly, be it by increasing or decreasing the producer
accuracy. Furthermore, and since the ground reference contained the boundaries of the
diﬀerent ﬁelds over the studied area, a ﬁeld wise classiﬁcation has been implemented and
compared to the pixel wise classiﬁcation, and proven to be more accurate for all classes.
These two maximum likelihood classiﬁcations used the same ENL for all pixels and for
all time points. Finally, several experimentations have been carried out, on the best way
to handle the ENL in maximum likelihood classiﬁcation. The pixel wise classiﬁcation
has been compared to another pixel wise classiﬁcation, but using a diﬀerent and speciﬁc
ENL for each diﬀerent time point. It has appeared that no signiﬁcant change can be
observed, which is certainly due to the high ENL on all images, and the rather small ENL
ﬂuctuation between diﬀerent acquisition dates. Another ﬁeld wise classiﬁcation has been
tried, for which the used ENL for classiﬁcation was calculated directly from the ﬁeld to
classify, for each diﬀerent date. This classiﬁcation has proven to be worse than the usual
ﬁeld wise classiﬁcation with the same ENL on all pixels and dates.
On the Foulum data set, which consists of one only fully polarimetric SAR measurement,
107
==PAGE:110==
the focus has been diﬀerent. Maximum likelihood classiﬁers have been trained and com-
pared on all the possible subsets of the covariance matrix that can be acquired by a SAR
device. Hence, all the single polarizations classiﬁers, the three possible combinations for
dual intensity classiﬁers, the three possible combinations for reduced Wishart classiﬁers
and the full Wishart classiﬁer have been trained and their classiﬁcation of the area com-
pared. It has appeared that in average, adding the diagonal elements to the intensities
for the HV-VV and HH-HV polarizations does not signiﬁcantly improve the classiﬁcation
accuracy, which means that the reduced Wishart classiﬁers on these polarizations did not
perform better than the respective dual intensity classiﬁers. However, the HH-VV reduced
Wishart classiﬁer performs way better than its dual intensity counterpart, highlighting
the high importance of the HH-VV oﬀdiagonal elements of the covariance matrix for
classiﬁcation. Moreover, a study has been performed to assess the importance of subdi-
viding classes presenting multi-modal behaviours for at least one of their attributes for
classiﬁcation. Due to the absence of merging procedure, the accuracy of single polariza-
tion classiﬁers dropped when adding the subdivision step. The dual intensity classiﬁers
had more or less the same accuracy, but the accuracy of the full Wishart classiﬁer and
the HH-VV reduced Wishart classiﬁer beneﬁted greatly from this step. It shows that
the subdivision step is important for maximum likelihood classiﬁers, but that they also
need a speciﬁc merging procedure, based on the distances between classes for the speciﬁc
classiﬁer.
Finally, several decompositions of fully polarimetric SAR data have been implemented and
compared: the H-α-anisotropy decomposition, the Freeman and Durden decomposition,
the Van Zyl decomposition, the Yamaguchi decomposition and the NNED decomposition.
The four last decompositions, while being diﬀerent, give very similar results. random
forest classiﬁers have been tried on the Foulum data set.
It has appeared that they
performed better than the full Wishart classiﬁers when using all the elements from the
covariance matrix, and that the subdivision of classes does not impact their accuracy. It
has also appeared that the random forest classiﬁers trained with the attributes of the four
last decompositions give extremely similar classiﬁcations and results, showing once again
the similarity between the decompositions. Between them all, the Freeman and Durden
decomposition gave (from only a little margin) the best classiﬁcation. Random forest
classiﬁers are a very useful tool, since they don’t need the user to check the distributions
of all attributes for all classes and subdiving them, which is an extremely long task.
Instead, they require the user to ensure that all classes have the same size in the training
set provided to the classiﬁer.
Further study on this subject could be done by comparing other classiﬁers with the ones
already implemented, on the same data sets. Numerous other classiﬁers are indeed use din
the literature for ground cover classiﬁcation, such as neural networks or support vector
machines. Another interesting study would be to compare the results of the classiﬁcation
using a classiﬁer trained only on the three diagonal elements and the two oﬀ-diagonal
elements of the HH-VV polarization of the covariance matrix to the classiﬁcation of a
classiﬁer trained on the whole covariance matrix.
